                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The seed plants today are represented by five distinct lineages: the species-rich angiosperms (flowering plants, approximately 352,000 species) and four gymnosperm lineages (which together comprise approximately 1,000 species and encompass cycads, <i>Ginkgo biloba</i>, conifers and gnetophytes). It is apparent from their long fossil record (dating back to the Late Devonian approximately 360 million years ago (Ma)) that considerably greater seed plant diversity existed in the past<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Rothwell, G. W. &amp; Scheckler, S. E. in Origin and Evolution of Gymnosperms (ed. Beck, C. B.) 85â134 (Columbia University Press, New York, 1988)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1097">1</a></sup>. Nevertheless, widespread extinctions among many gymnosperm lineages mean that todayâs gymnosperms are only a relic of their former diversity, and this has presented a major challenge for reconstructing evolutionary relationships between the extant lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lu, Y., Ran, J. H., Guo, D. M., Yang, Z. Y. &amp; Wang, X. Q. Phylogeny and divergence times of gymnosperms inferred from single-copy nuclear genes. PLoS ONE 9, e107679 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1101">2</a></sup>. Probably the most controversial outstanding question in plant evolution is the phylogenetic position of gnetophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Doyle, J. A. Molecular and fossil evidence on the origin of angiosperms. Annu. Rev. Earth Planet. Sci. 40, 301â326 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1105">3</a></sup> (comprising the genera <i>Gnetum</i>, <i>Welwitschia</i> and <i>Ephedra</i>, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig1">1</a>) in relation to the other seed plant lineages. Apparent morphological similarities with angiosperms, such as vessel-like water-conducting cells, double fertilization and leaf morphologies with reticulate venation, have historically led to the proposition that gnetophytes form a group that is sister to angiosperms (termed the âAnthophyte hypothesisâ)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Doyle, J. A. &amp; Donoghue, M. J. Seed plant phylogeny and the origin of angiosperms: an experimental cladistic approach. Bot. Rev. 52, 321â431 (1986)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1122">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Crane, P. R. Phylogenetic analysis of seed plants and the origin of angiosperms. Ann. Mo. Bot. Gard. 72, 716â793 (1985)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1125">5</a></sup>. That hypothesis has, however, largely been rejected by molecular phylogenetic data and a deeper understanding of the developmental pathways that lead to similar morphological features. Nevertheless, the use of molecular data has also been problematic in inferring the exact phylogenetic position of gnetophytes, with topologies differing depending on the type of sequence data (for example, plastid versus nuclear genes, nucleotide versus amino acid data) and analytical approach used (for example, maximum parsimony, maximum likelihood, Bayesian, multispecies coalescent based methods)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mathews, S. Phylogenetic relationships among seed plants: persistent questions and the limits of molecular data. Am. J. Bot. 96, 228â236 (2009)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1129">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, X. Q. &amp; Ran, J. H. Evolution and biogeography of gymnosperms. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 75, 24â40 (2014)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1129_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 4859â4868 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1132">8</a></sup>. Consequently, several possible hypotheses have been put forward that place gnetophytes as sister to (1) Pinaceae (âGnepineâ hypothesis); (2) cupressophytes (âGnecupâ hypothesis); (3) all conifers (âGnetiferâ hypothesis); (4) all other gymnosperms; or (5) all seed plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, Z. et al. Single-copy genes as molecular markers for phylogenomic studies in seed plants. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 1130â1147 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1136">9</a></sup>. Currently, the emerging consensus, based on both older and more recent studies, and recently released data from the 1KP initiative (see <a href="https://sites.google.com/a/ualberta.ca/onekp/">https://sites.google.com/a/ualberta.ca/onekp/</a>, and Wickett et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 4859â4868 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1148">8</a></sup>), indicates that gnetophytes are sister to, or within, the conifers.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Morphological variation and geographical distribution of gnetophytes and some other gymnosperms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Morphological variation and geographical distribution of gnetophytes and some other gymnosperms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Top, left to right, female cones of <i>G. montanum</i>, male cones of <i>W. mirabilis</i> and female cones of <i>E. equisetina</i>. Scale bars, 5âcm. Bottom, pantropical distribution of the three gnetophyte genera, compared with three conifer species that are most abundant at higher latitudes and altitudes. The range of genomes sizes (1C-values) found in the three genera comprising gnetophytes and the three conifer species are also shown (data taken from <a href="http://data.kew.org/cvalues/">http://data.kew.org/cvalues/</a> and unpublished data).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>So far, the availability of whole genome sequences for gymnosperms has been limited to conifers (specifically to Pinaceae)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Warren, R. L. et al. Improved white spruce (Picea glauca) genome assemblies and annotation of large gene families of conifer terpenoid and phenolic defense metabolism. Plant J. 83, 189â212 (2015)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1191">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1191_1">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1191_2">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Sequence of the sugar pine megagenome. Genetics 204, 1613â1626 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1194">13</a></sup> and <i>G. biloba</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1200">14</a></sup>, with no whole genome assemblies available for the two remaining major seed plant lineagesâcycads and gnetophytes. This deficiency, together with the conflicting phylogenetic evidence for relationships among these groups, is impeding our understanding of genome evolution across all seed plants. Here, we present a high-quality draft genome of <i>Gnetum montanum</i>, the first for gnetophytes. The availability of this genome, as well as survey sequence data and transcriptome data from other vascular plants (including novel data from gnetophytes <i>Ephedra</i> and <i>Welwitschia</i>), enables us to compare genomic characters with <i>G. biloba</i>, conifers, angiosperms and non-seed plants. Comparisons within gymnosperms, and between gymnosperms and angiosperms, highlight the unique nature of the <i>Gnetum</i> genome, providing new insights into patterns of genome divergence across seed plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Genome assembly and annotation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Genome assembly and annotation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>The genome of <i>G. montanum</i> (2<i>n</i>â=â44) is small compared with other gymnosperms (flow cytometry, 4.2âGb/1C; k-mer analysis, 4.11âGb), and is highly heterozygous and rich in repeats (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">1aâc</a> and Supplementary Information). To overcome problems caused by repeats and heterozygosity, we generated deep coverage (~302Ã, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">1</a>) Illumina sequence data and applied a novel genome assembly strategy (Supplementary Information and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">2</a>) to assemble 4.07âGb of sequence (contig N50 sizeâ=â25.02âkb, scaffold N50 sizeâ=â475.17âkb, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">2</a>), to which &gt;99% of genome reads, &gt;90% expressed sequence tags (ESTs) and &gt;99% of bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) were mapped (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">1d,e</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">3</a> and Note 3).</p><p>A total of 27,491 protein-coding genes were predicted from this assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Information), 97% of which were supported by orthology (&gt;50% coverage of a high-scoring segment pair, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">3a</a>) with existing protein sequences and/or RNA-seq data from multiple tissues (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">5</a>). A BUSCO (Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs) analysis to assess the quality of the genome and annotation completeness suggested that 81% of the genes have been recovered (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">6</a>). Unlike conifer genomes, which contain numerous pseudogenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Garcia-Gil, M. R. Evolutionary aspects of functional and pseudogene members of the phytochrome gene family in Scots pine. J. Mol. Evol. 67, 222â232 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1268">15</a></sup> (for example, 8,328 in <i>Picea abies</i>, 13,550 in <i>Pinus taeda</i>), many fewer were found in the <i>G. montanum</i> genome (3,122, Supplementary Information). The read depth distribution across genic regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">3b</a>) suggested little sequence redundancy caused by heterozygosity (see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">3c</a> for further confirmation of gene assembly quality).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Repetitive sequence dynamics"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Repetitive sequence dynamics</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Repetitive sequences have been shown to account for the major component of all gymnosperm genomes that have been sequenced to date<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1296">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1296_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Sequence of the sugar pine megagenome. Genetics 204, 1613â1626 (2016)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1296_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1299">14</a></sup>, with diverse and ancient transposable elements (TEs), especially LTR retrotransposons (LTR-RTs), being particularly prevalent. Overall, the repetitive element content of <i>G. montanum</i> was also high (85.9%) and dominated by LTR-RTs (especially <i>gypsy</i>-like elements), which constituted 77.4% of the genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Information). The genome assembly of <i>G. montanum</i> is likely to be sufficient to represent most of the LTR-RTs, since their length is typically around 25 kb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1316">16</a></sup>, and 90% of the scaffolds are larger than 34âkb. Phylogenetic reconstructions of the reverse transcriptase domains of LTR-RTs in <i>G. montanum</i> and <i>P. taeda</i> revealed that most of the <i>gypsy</i>- and <i>copia</i>-like elements in <i>G. montanum</i> were restricted to just a few clades, representing only a small minority of the diversity encountered in <i>P. taeda</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Information).</p><p>Comparative analyses of repeats identified by RepeatExplorer using survey sequence data from multiple gnetophytes (<i>G</i><i>. montanum, Gnetum gnemon</i>, <i>Welwitschia mirabilis</i> and <i>Ephedra altissima</i>) and <i>P. taeda</i> revealed substantial differences in the abundance of the major repeat classes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">5a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">9</a> and Supplementary Information). Further, the majority of individual repeat types (repeat clusters in RepeatExplorer) were shown to be species specific (containing Illumina reads from just one species, data not shown). The species-specific nature of the repeat profiles probably reflects the long estimated divergence times between species (for example, the two <i>Gnetum</i> species are likely to have diverged between approximately 25 Ma and 75 Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Won, H. &amp; Renner, S. S. Dating dispersal and radiation in the gymnosperm Gnetum (Gnetales)âclock calibration when outgroup relationships are uncertain. Syst. Biol. 55, 610â622 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1369">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hou, C., Humphreys, A. M., Thureborn, O. &amp; Rydin, C. New insights into the evolutionary history of Gnetum (Gnetales). Taxon 64, 239â253 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1372">18</a></sup>.</p><p>Previously, it was reported from conifers and <i>G. biloba</i> that LTR-RTs have accumulated steadily over the last approximately 25 Ma, especially between 16 and 24 Ma, a process contributing to their large genome sizes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1382">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1385">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1388">14</a></sup>. This interpretation is consistent with the data here (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">10</a>), which show that most LTR-RTs in conifers are intact (solo LTR/intact LTR ratio ranged from 0.16:1 to 0.72:1, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">10</a>). It is notable that the solo LTR/intact LTR ratio was substantially higher in <i>G. montanum</i> (~1.94:1), which together with its small genome and similar profile of accumulation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">5b</a>) suggest higher frequencies of LTR-RT elimination than amplification compared with <i>G. biloba</i> and conifers.</p><p>Most angiosperm genomes analysed to date have far fewer ancient repeats and less divergent LTR-RT subsets than conifers and <i>G. biloba</i>, presumably because of more efficient elimination and replacement processes operating within these angiosperm genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Leitch, A. R. &amp; Leitch, I. J. Ecological and genetic factors linked to contrasting genome dynamics in seed plants. New Phytol. 194, 629â646 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1414">19</a></sup> (for example, in <i>Oryza sativa</i> the half-life of LTR-RTs is estimated to be less than five million years<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Ma, J., Devos, K. M. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. Analyses of LTR-retrotransposon structures reveal recent and rapid genomic DNA loss in rice. Genome Res. 14, 860â869 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1421">20</a></sup>, leading to âgenome turnoverâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lim, K. Y. et al. Sequence of events leading to near-complete genome turnover in allopolyploid Nicotiana within five million years. New Phytol. 175, 756â763 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1425">21</a></sup>). However, an exception to this pattern has been observed in <i>Amborella trichopoda</i>. The genome of this species is considered to have retained many features that were likely to have been present in the ancestral angiosperm genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1433">22</a></sup>. It is notable that its repeat content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Stevens, K. A. et al. Sequence of the sugar pine megagenome. Genetics 204, 1613â1626 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1437">13</a></sup> and lower abundance of intact LTR-RTs (solo LTR/intact LTR ratioâ=â2.43/1.0; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">10</a>) is similar to that observed in <i>G. montanum</i>. These observations suggest that neither <i>A. trichopoda</i> nor <i>G. montanum</i> genomes have experienced recent, extensive (retro) transposon activity, although they continue to eliminate repetitive sequences. Both these species seem to differ from conifers and <i>G. biloba</i> with respect to the dynamics of repeat accumulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1457">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1460">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1463">14</a></sup>, and from other angiosperms in terms of the levels of repeat amplification/removal.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="Intron morphologies"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">Intron morphologies</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>Although intron size has been positively correlated with genome size across eukaryotes as a whole<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Vinogradov, A. E. Intron-genome size relationship on a large evolutionary scale. J. Mol. Evol. 49, 376â384 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1475">23</a></sup>, this trend does not translate well across broad and some narrow taxonomic distances in seed plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2a</a>). Previous studies of <i>G. biloba</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1484">14</a></sup> and conifers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1488">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1491">12</a></sup> have reported larger introns than angiosperms, probably arising from the long-term, steady amplification of LTR-RTs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2b</a>), as also observed here, where LTR-RTs account for 51% and 59% of the large intron sequences in <i>P. taeda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i>, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">12</a>). The evolution of these large introns may have arisen from similar repeat accumulation processes that are operating across the genome as a whole.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparative analysis of seed plant intron morphologies."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Comparative analysis of seed plant intron morphologies.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Intron length distributions and genome sizes (1C-values, depicted by the relative circle size) are shown for nine representative seed plants. <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>; <i>G. max</i>, <i>Glycine max</i>. <b>b</b>, Distribution of sequence divergence for four types of TEs in introns of <i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>G. montanum</i>, <i>P. taeda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i>. The data show that TEs in <i>G. montanum</i> and <i>A. trichopoda</i> are more diverse than in <i>P. taeda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i>. The last two species also show a peak at around 10% sequence divergence probably reflecting a pulse of LTR-RT expansions. <b>c</b>â<b>e</b>, Comparison of orthologous introns between <i>P. taeda</i> (Pta) versus <i>G. biloba</i> (Gbi) (<b>c</b>), <i>P. taeda</i> versus <i>G. montanum</i> (Gmo) (<b>d</b>), and <i>G. montanum</i> versus <i>A. trichopoda</i> (Atr) (<b>e</b>). Two orthologous intron sets that differed more than twofold in length were examined: âshortâ introns, 0.5â3âkb; and âlongâ introns, 6 kb. Orthologous introns that were long in one species were also found to be long in the other species of the pair. Analysis of the TEs in orthologous introns showed the long introns of <i>G. montanum</i> and <i>A. trichopoda</i> carried a high proportion of LINEs, contributing to intron expansion. In contrast, <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> LTR-RT elements contributed most to intron expansion in <i>P. taeda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>When comparing these observations with introns of <i>G. montanum</i>, it is apparent that their introns are substantially smaller (minimum, mean and maximum intron lengths) than those of <i>P. taeda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2a</a>, see also the statistics test in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">11</a>). In addition, the repeat composition of <i>G</i>. <i>montanum</i>âs introns is dominated by both long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs) and LTR-RTs, rather than predominantly LTR-RTs, as in conifers and <i>G. biloba</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">12</a>). The correlation between smaller intron sizes and smaller genome size in <i>G. montanum</i> compared with conifers and <i>G. biloba</i> may reflect the repeat dynamic processes operating across its genome as a whole. In contrast, the variable length distributions of introns in angiosperms suggest that the evolution of repeats in their introns do not necessarily reflect the repeat dynamics observed across the rest of their genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wendel, J. F. et al. Intron size and genome size in plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 19, 2346â2352 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1669">24</a></sup>. In the highly dynamic repetitive genome of <i>Zea mays</i>, the profile of repeats across the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Schnable, P. S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1676">25</a></sup> and within the whole intron set (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">6a</a>) both suggest many recent insertions. However, in <i>A. trichopoda</i>, the intron sizes are larger overall, and the genome size smaller than in <i>Z. mays</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2a,b</a>). In addition, an analysis of introns in <i>A. trichopoda</i> and <i>G. montanum</i> highlighted a closer similarity to each other (in terms of length distributions, repeat composition and divergence) than either species has to conifers and <i>G. biloba</i>, despite a 4.8-fold difference in their genome sizes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2a,b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>Previous comparisons of orthologous introns have led to the suggestion that the expansion of introns occurred early in the evolutionary history of conifers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1712">12</a></sup>. Comparisons of orthologous introns (with identical adjacent exons) between <i>P. taeda</i> and <i>G. biloba</i> showed that introns identified as being long (&gt;6âkb) in <i>P. taeda</i> were also typically long in their orthologues in <i>G. biloba</i>, containing, in both cases, abundant LTR-RTs (both <i>gypsy</i>- and <i>copia</i>-like elements, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2c</a>). These features were likely to have been present in their most recent common ancestor (MRCA). Using similar approaches to analyse the length and repeat content of 4,348 orthologous introns of <i>G. montanum</i> shared with <i>P. taeda</i> (Supplementary Information) highlighted notable differences. The length of exons remained similar, but a substantial fraction of orthologous genes had longer introns in <i>P. taeda</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">6b</a>). The introns identified as âshortâ in <i>P</i>. <i>taeda</i> comprised approximately 4% repeats, rising to approximately 56% in âlongâ introns, largely through the accumulation of LTR-RTs (especially <i>copia</i> elements) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">13</a>). In contrast, introns in <i>G. montanum</i> that are orthologous to the âlongâ introns of <i>P. taeda</i> (36% of introns analysed) showed high proportions of LINEs. As with comparisons of all introns, pairwise comparisons of orthologous introns in <i>G. montanum</i> and <i>A. trichopoda</i> again showed some similarities in their introns, with both species having abundant LINEs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig2">2e</a>). Collectively, these data reveal a different repeat dynamic within introns of <i>G. montanum</i> compared with the other gymnosperms.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="(âLack ofâ) Whole genome duplication"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">(âLack ofâ) Whole genome duplication</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>All angiosperms are reported to have undergone at least one round of ancient whole genome duplication (WGD), and in many lineages WGDs are recurrent and ongoing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Van de Peer, Y., Mizrachi, E. &amp; Marchal, K. The evolutionary significance of polyploidy. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 411â424 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1793">26</a></sup>. In addition, a WGD event has been proposed at the base of all seed plants approximately 341 Ma (<i>zeta</i> WGD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Ancestral polyploidy in seed plants and angiosperms. Nature 473, 97â100 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1800">27</a></sup>), although the underlying evidence for these two ancient WGD events has been recently questioned<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ruprecht, C. et al. Revisiting ancestral polyploidy in plants. Sci. Adv. 3, e1603195 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1804">28</a></sup>. In gymnosperms, WGDs have been reported for conifers, <i>G. biloba</i> and cycads (a likely shared WGD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1812">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv. 1, e1501084 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1815">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Roodt, D. et al. Evidence for an ancient whole genome duplication in the cycad lineage. PLoS ONE 12, e0184454 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1818">30</a></sup>. Although recent polyploidy seems common in extant <i>Ephedra</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Wu, H. et al. A high frequency of allopolyploid speciation in the gymnospermous genus Ephedra and its possible association with some biological and ecological features. Mol. Ecol. 25, 1192â1210 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1824">31</a></sup>, evidence for ancient WGDs in gnetophytes is missing (Supplementary Information and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">7</a>), except for a WGD in <i>Welwitchia</i> which is likely to have occurred after the divergence of its lineage from that leading to <i>Ephedra</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">7</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Li, Z. et al. Early genome duplications in conifers and other seed plants. Sci. Adv. 1, e1501084 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1841">29</a></sup>. If indeed the ancient zeta WGD is shared by all seed plants, the absence of evidence for this event in gnetophytes is best explained by their faster rates of gene evolution than other gymnosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hajibabaei, M., Xia, J. &amp; Drouin, G. Seed plant phylogeny: gnetophytes are derived conifers and a sister group to Pinaceae. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 40, 208â217 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1845">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="De La Torre, A. R., Li, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Ingvarsson, P. K. Contrasting rates of molecular evolution and patterns of selection among gymnosperms and flowering plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1363â1377 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1848">33</a></sup>, erasing all evidence of this more than 300 million year old event (Supplementary Information and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">7</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Organization of functional protein domains"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Organization of functional protein domains</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>To characterize the patterns of functional diversification in gene domains across land plants, we used principal component analysis (PCA) to analyse the number of pfam domains (conserved protein domains) in multiple species (Supplementary Information and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">13</a>). Our approach showed that angiosperms formed a discrete cluster that was separate from the gymnosperms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig3">3a</a>), with <i>G. montanum</i> being an outlier. Indeed, heatmaps compiled from the pfam data that contributed most (top 10%) to PCA1 and PCA2 showed that <i>G. montanum</i> formed a clade with the lycophyte <i>Selaginella moellendorffii</i> and the moss <i>Physcomitrella patens</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig3">3b</a>), but the non-gnetophyte gymnosperms formed a separate clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genome-wide analysis to show the contrasting diversification of functional protein domains across land plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Genome-wide analysis to show the contrasting diversification of functional protein domains across land plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, PCA analysis of the occurrence and number of pfam domains in multiple orthologous genes across land plants. Plotting PC1 against PC2 reveals that monocots and eudicots cluster together, as do conifers with <i>G. biloba</i>; the remaining species are separate from these clusters. <b>b</b>, Heatmaps reveal the ancestral coding repertories shared by <i>S. moellendorffii</i> and <i>G. montanum</i>. Different patterns of expansion and contraction of the pfam domains are seen for other gymnosperms and angiosperms (see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">7</a> for species name list and corresponding abbreviations).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given the distinct distributions of <i>G. montanum</i>, non-gnetophyte gymnosperms and angiosperms in the PCA analysis, the data suggest that significant functional diversification of the conserved protein domains has occurred since these major lineages split. It may be surprising given the long divergence times (approximately 300 Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lu, Y., Ran, J. H., Guo, D. M., Yang, Z. Y. &amp; Wang, X. Q. Phylogeny and divergence times of gymnosperms inferred from single-copy nuclear genes. PLoS ONE 9, e107679 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1932">2</a></sup>, that <i>G. biloba</i> and conifers retain similar conserved domain organizations (with similar eigenvector values). This could reflect their relatively low substitution rates (on average seven times lower) compared with angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="De La Torre, A. R., Li, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Ingvarsson, P. K. Contrasting rates of molecular evolution and patterns of selection among gymnosperms and flowering plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1363â1377 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1939">33</a></sup>.</p><p>An analysis of the pfam domain expansions that contributed most to the PCA1 and PCA2 distributions among angiosperms (except <i>A. trichopoda</i>) included genes associated with flower and organ development (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM3">15</a>). In contrast, non-gnetophyte gymnosperms showed large-scale specific expansions of pfam domains in genes associated with defence and secondary metabolism, as previously suggested (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM4">16</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Warren, R. L. et al. Improved white spruce (Picea glauca) genome assemblies and annotation of large gene families of conifer terpenoid and phenolic defense metabolism. Plant J. 83, 189â212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1955">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1958">11</a></sup>. The clustering of <i>G. montanum</i> with non-seed plants in the heatmap (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig3">3b</a>) was a surprise, and may indicate the approach has identified proteins that have diverged very little since the MRCA of seed plants. Nevertheless, such an explanation is at odds with the hypothesis that the genes of gnetophytes have diverged rapidly, given their comparatively high substitution rate compared with other gymnosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="De La Torre, A. R., Li, Z., Van de Peer, Y. &amp; Ingvarsson, P. K. Contrasting rates of molecular evolution and patterns of selection among gymnosperms and flowering plants. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1363â1377 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1969">33</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Growth form (shrubs and lianas) and leaf morphology"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Growth form (shrubs and lianas) and leaf morphology</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Gnetophytes differ from other extant gymnosperms in growth form, with the unusual and distinct form of <i>Welwitschia</i>, the shrub habit of <i>Ephedra</i> and the shrub and liana habit and specialized leaf morphologies of <i>Gnetum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Frohlich, M. W. &amp; Chase, M. W. After a dozen years of progress the origin of angiosperms is still a great mystery. Nature 450, 1184â1189 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d24949e1989">34</a></sup>. Cellulose synthase (<i>Ces</i>A) and cellulose synthase-like (<i>Csl</i>) genes are considered to play a role in influencing the biomechanical properties of the cell<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Popper, Z. A. et al. Evolution and diversity of plant cell walls: from algae to flowering plants. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 62, 567â590 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2000">35</a></sup>, hence potentially the distinctive growth forms of gnetophytes are associated with the divergence of these genes. To explore this hypothesis, <i>Ces</i>A and <i>Csl</i> family members were examined in <i>G. montanum</i> and compared with those in other seed plants. The total number of <i>Ces</i>A and <i>Csl</i> family members ranged about threefold among the seed plants analysed (<i>P. abies</i>, <i>P. taeda</i>, <i>A. trichopoda</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>O. sativa</i>). However, only <i>G. montanum</i> showed a large expansion of the <i>Csl</i>B/H gene subfamily (to 20 genes, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">17</a>), involving tandem duplications (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">9</a>), and accounting for two-thirds of its total <i>Csl</i> gene repertoire. Furthermore, transcriptome analysis showed that these <i>Csl</i>B/H genes were differentially expressed in leaves, stems and roots of <i>G. montanum</i>, supporting an association with distinct growth forms and leaf morphologies (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">9</a>). In contrast, all other species analysed, including <i>Welwitschia</i> and <i>Ephedra</i>, were seen to have only one to six <i>Csl</i>B/H genes (at least based on transcriptome analysis) (Supplementary Information, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM4">16</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><p>Another gene family associated with leaf morphology and development is the <i>WOX</i> (<i>WUSCHEL-related homeobox</i>) family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nakata, M. et al. Roles of the middle domain-specific WUSCHEL-RELATED HOMEOBOX genes in early development of leaves in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 24, 519â535 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2085">36</a></sup>. Recent studies have shown that the conserved family members WOX3 and WOX4, which play a role in leaf development, show diffuse <i>WOX3</i> expression at the leaf bases of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and <i>Gnetum</i>, with such patterns being associated with the distinctive reticulate venation observed in their leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Nardmann, J. &amp; Werr, W. Symplesiomorphies in the WUSCHEL clade suggest that the last common ancestor of seed plants contained at least four independent stem cell niches. New Phytol. 199, 1081â1092 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2099">37</a></sup>. Two unusual paralogues, GgWOXX and GgWOXY, were previously reported to occur only in gnetophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Nardmann, J. &amp; Werr, W. Symplesiomorphies in the WUSCHEL clade suggest that the last common ancestor of seed plants contained at least four independent stem cell niches. New Phytol. 199, 1081â1092 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2103">37</a></sup>, and this is confirmed here in phylogenetic reconstructions of gene family members (Supplementary Information and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">10</a>). These paralogues are unlikely to have arisen by <i>Gnetum</i>-specific gene amplifications, as this would group them with other <i>Gnetum</i> paralogues. Alternatively, these genes may correspond to ancestral seed plant sequences that have been lost in other plant lineages. Potentially the different patterns of gene loss, retention and amplification compared with other gymnosperms may be associated with their distinctive growth forms.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Vessels"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Vessels</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The presence of vessel-like water-conducting cells, morphologically distinct from tracheids, is another feature that sets gnetophytes apart from other gymnosperms. However, there has been longstanding debate whether gnetophyte âvesselsâ are homologous to the âvesselsâ of angiosperms. In angiosperms, VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN (VND) proteins <i>VND1-</i>7 are members of the <i>NAC</i> domain class of transcription factors, <i>VND7</i> being a master regulator of vessel formation in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Yamaguchi, M. et al. VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN7 directly regulates the expression of a broad range of genes for xylem vessel formation. Plant J. 66, 579â590 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2135">38</a></sup>, and <i>VND1-6</i> being upstream regulators of <i>VND7</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Endo, H. et al. Multiple classes of transcription factors regulate the expression of VASCULAR-RELATED NAC-DOMAIN7, a master switch of xylem vessel differentiation. Plant Cell Physiol. 56, 242â254 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2145">39</a></sup>. Although five <i>NAC</i> domain genes were identified in the genome of <i>G. montanum</i>, no orthologues of <i>VND</i>7 or <i>VND1-3</i> in the sister clade were identified, consistent with previous analyses of other gymnosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2162">12</a></sup>, and suggesting that these proteins are restricted to angiosperms (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">11</a>). Nevertheless, <i>Gnetum</i> does share the <i>VND4-6</i> clade with angiosperms and other gymnosperms. Furthermore, <i>A. trichopoda</i>, which lacks angiosperm vessels, also lacks orthologues of <i>VND1-3</i>, but it does have <i>VND7</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">11</a>), indicating that the ability to form vessels may have occurred after angiosperms diverged. Taken together, these data suggest a greater dependency of vessel development on <i>VND1-3</i> than is apparent from experiments on <i>A. thaliana</i>. The most parsimonious explanation of our data is that angiosperm vessel formation requires genes from the <i>VND7</i> clade (and potentially its sister clade <i>VND1-3</i>), and that gymnosperms, including gnetophytes, which lack sequences from both these clades cannot form structures that are homologous to angiosperm vessels. Such an interpretation supports Carlquistâs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Carlquist, S. Wood, bark and stem anatomy of New World species of Gnetum. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 120, 1â19 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2201">40</a></sup> morphological interpretations of vessels. It is therefore most likely that different molecular mechanisms underpin the origin and development of vessels in <i>Gnetum</i> and angiosperms. Indeed, these new molecular data support the hypothesis based on morphological studies that <i>Gnetum</i> vessels are actually more closely related to conifer tracheids than angiosperm vessels and that vessels in the two groups are convergent characters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Carlquist, S. Wood, bark and stem anatomy of New World species of Gnetum. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 120, 1â19 (1996)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2211">40</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Water stress"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Water stress</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Extant species of <i>Gnetum</i> are unusual among gymnosperms in being restricted to warm, mesic habitats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Ickert-Bond, S. M. &amp; Renner, S. S. The Gnetales: recent insights on their morphology, reproductive biology, chromosome numbers, biogeography, and divergence times. J. Syst. Evol. 54, 1â16 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2226">41</a></sup>; this contrasts to conifers that are adapted to cold and water-stressed environments. An analysis of genes involved in water and cold stress revealed some substantial differences between conifers and <i>Gnetum</i>. The late embryogenesis abundant protein (LEA) gene family encodes crucial proteins that are involved in protecting plants from desiccation or osmotic stresses associated with low temperature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hundertmark, M. &amp; Hincha, D. K. LEA (late embryogenesis abundant) proteins and their encoding genes in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Genom. 9, 1â22 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2233">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Gao, J. &amp; Lan, T. Functional characterization of the late embryogenesis abundant (LEA) protein gene family from Pinus tabuliformis (Pinaceae) in Escherichia coli. Sci. Rep. 6, 19467 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2236">43</a></sup>. An analysis of LEA family members suggests that some members have been reduced in number in <i>Gnetum</i> or expanded in conifers (for example, LEA-3), or lost completely in <i>Gnetum</i> (LEA-4, 5, 6). In addition, dehydrins, which play a role in the response to cold/drought<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Richard, S., Morency, M. J., Drevet, C., Jouanin, L. &amp; Seguin, A. Isolation and characterization of a dehydrin gene from white spruce induced upon wounding, drought and cold stresses. Plant Mol. Biol. 43, 1â10 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2247">44</a></sup>, had only two members in <i>G. montanum</i>, compared with 38 in <i>P. abies</i>, 28 in <i>P. taeda</i> and 3â15 in angiosperms (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">19</a>). Further analysis of the <i>G. montanum</i> genome also revealed relatively few gene family members of the AP2 domain containing protein families, which are involved in the cold stress response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Chinnusamy, V., Zhu, J. &amp; Zhu, J. K. Cold stress regulation of gene expression in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 12, 444â451 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2267">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Du, C. et al. Dynamic transcriptome analysis reveals AP2/ERF transcription factors responsible for cold stress in rapeseed (Brassica napus L.). Mol. Genet. Genom. 291, 1053â1067 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2270">46</a></sup>, and glutathione peroxidase and glutathione S-transferase families, involved in the oxidant stress response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Roxas, V. P., Smith, R. K. Jr., Allen, E. R. &amp; Allen, R. D. Overexpression of glutathione S-transferase/glutathioneperoxidase enhances the growth of transgenic tobacco seedlings during stress. Nat. Biotechnol. 15, 988â991 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2274">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Zhao, J. et al. Global transcriptional profiling of a cold-tolerant rice variety under moderate cold stress reveals different cold stress response mechanisms. Physiol. Plant 154, 381â394 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2277">48</a></sup>. Taken together, these data appear consistent with the hypothesis that the ecological shift to a warm, wet forest habitat is associated with a relaxation of selection pressure on genes associated with water stress and low temperature.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Conclusion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Conclusion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Here, we have described the assembly, annotation and comparative analysis of the first gnetophyte genome, namely that of <i>G. montanum</i>. Its genome is particularly enigmatic given a phylogenetic position within or sister to conifers. It also carries genomic peculiarities that may reflect its morphological and ecological uniqueness amongst gymnosperms. Comparisons of these genome features with the genomes of conifers and <i>G. biloba</i> provide opportunities to predict the nature and direction of genomic change accompanying the evolution of the lineage leading to <i>Gnetum</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#Fig4">4</a>). Assuming that gnetophytes do indeed form a clade that is sister to, or within, the conifers, the following genomic features can be predicted to have been present in the MRCA of the gymnosperms, as observed in <i>G. biloba</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2303">14</a></sup> and conifers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Neale, D. B. et al. Decoding the massive genome of loblolly pine using haploid DNA and novel assembly strategies. Genome Biol. 15, R59 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2308">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2311">12</a></sup>: (1) a large genome size (1Câ&gt;â10âGb) comprised predominantly of a heterogeneous set of large numbers of LTR-RTs associated with low levels of repeat deletion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Guan, R. et al. Draft genome of the living fossil Ginkgo biloba. GigaScience 5, 49 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2315">14</a></sup>; (2) long introns predominantly shaped by insertions of LTR-RTs (<i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i> elements); (3) pfam domains that show a profile distinct from angiosperms. If this is so, and assuming a common ancestry of gnetophytes and conifers, these genomic characters, or their signatures, have subsequently been lost or diverged considerably in the lineage leading to <i>Gnetum</i>. This most likely involved the following genomic processes: (1) genome downsizing, leading to the relatively (for a gymnosperm) small genomes of <i>Gnetum</i> species (1Câ=â2.25â4.11âGb). This is supported by the high ratio of solo LTR/intact LTR-RTs observed in the genome of <i>Gnetum</i> compared with conifers, and is indicative of the activity of recombination-based processes, which can eliminate DNA from the genome. Similar processes leading to genome downsizing have also been reported in many angiosperms, resulting in small genomes despite the occurrence of multiple rounds of polyploidy detected in many lineages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wendel, J. F. The wondrous cycles of polyploidy in plants. Am. J. Bot. 102, 1753â1756 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d24949e2335">49</a></sup>; (2) reduction in the size of introns in <i>G. montanum</i> and a replacement of many of the LTR-RTs repeats with LINEs to give rise to introns that are more similar to those of, for instance, <i>A. trichopoda</i> than to other gymnosperms; (3) elevated rates of sequence divergence causing the erosion of a hypothesised shared seed-plant WGD event and leading to a pattern of pfam domains, which is distinct from the remaining gymnosperms; (4) expansion and contraction of specific gene families associated with adaptation to new ecologies.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Prediction of patterns of genome divergence across seed plants."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Prediction of patterns of genome divergence across seed plants.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-017-0097-2/MediaObjects/41477_2017_97_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The origin and evolution of distinctive genomic features observed in the <i>G. montanum</i> genome are inferred, assuming a phylogenetic placement of gnetophytes as sister to, or within, conifers. The predicted features shared by respective lineages are marked by coloured circles. Whole genome duplication (WGD) events (red star) and a putative WGD event (grey stars) are shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>The sequenced <i>G. montanum</i> is a single mature female individual growing naturally in Fairy Lake Botanical Garden, Shenzhen, China. Genome sequences were generated using an Illumina platform and assembled with a novel hierarchical assembly strategy. Gene annotations were determined by integrating results from both de novo prediction approaches and alignment-based methods based on orthology and transcriptomic data. RNA-seq was performed using an Illumina platform. All methods and bioinformatic analyses are detailed in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Life Sciences Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-017-0097-2#MOESM2">Life Sciences Reporting Summary</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Data availability</h3><p>The <i>G. montanum</i> genome project has been deposited at the NCBI under the BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA339497/">PRJNA339497</a>. The whole genome sequencing data were deposited in the Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under the accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRX2052734&amp;cmd=DetailsSearch">SRX2052734</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2098865">SRX2098865</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2099144">SRX2099144</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2114825">SRX2114825</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2114827">SRX2114827</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2134147">SRX2134147</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2134160">SRX2134160</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2134177">SRX2134177</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2134180">SRX2134180</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2134596">SRX2134596</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/?term=SRX2134624">SRX2134624</a>. The <i>G. montanum</i> assemblies, gene sequences and annotation data are also available at the DRYAD website. The data or related program scripts that support the findings of this study are available from the corresponding author upon request.</p></div></div></section>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Xiao-Ming+Wang" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide">Â </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Xiao-Ming+Wang%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>T.W. and X.M.W. conceived and initiated the study, managing the gnetophytes (<i>Gnetum</i>, <i>Welwitschia</i>, <i>Ephedra</i>) genome sequencing project. T.W. designed the major scientific objectives and led the manuscript preparation together with A.R.L., I.J.L., J.B.Z, L.J.K and Y.V.d.P. The collaboration between groups was close in all aspects of the project. T.W., Z.M.L., L.L., A.R.L., I.J.L. and Z.J.L. are joint first authors, H.P.X., Y.B.G., Yang Liu, L.Y.C. and W.C.W. are joint second authors. Z.M.L., J.B.Z., J.L., Yan Liu performed the genome assembly and annotation; H.P.X., L.L., L.Y.C., L.M., X.R.Y. contributed to the RNA-seq and corresponding analysis. A.R.L., I.J.L. and W.C.W. coordinated the <i>RepeatExplorer</i> analysis in gnetophytes and contributed to the design of the analysis for investigating the dynamics of genome evolution. Z.M.L., J.B.Z., L.L., F.L., H.M.L., T.W., A.R.L., I.J.L., W.X. and Yan Liu participated in the analyses of LTR-RTs and comparisons of introns. R.L., T.W., Y.V.d.P., Z.L., Z.J.L. and Z.M.L. were involved in the WGD determination; M.L., L.L., J.B.Z., J.Y., T.W., L.Z., Y.B.G. and S.H.D. conducted PCA analysis of pfam domains. J.B.Z., T.W., J.L., L.L., L.J.K., Y. Lei and Z.M.L. performed the analysis investigating the divergence of gene families. J.L.H., P.L., Q.M., Yang Liu and G.Q.Z. contributed to the analysis of pseudogenes; Q.F.W., S.H.L. and S.Z.Z. helped with the collecting of <i>Welwitschia</i> and <i>Ephedra</i>. Y.Y. provided experimental information on the taxonomic identity of the species used for genome sequencing and collated the distribution records of gnetophytes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
